        </td>

        </th>

         7,669.28 ha
        </td>
       </tr>

      <p>
       <b>
        Toledo
       </b>
       (
       <small>
        Spanish:
       </small>
       <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">
        <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Spanish" title="Help:IPA/Spanish">
         [toˈleðo]
        </a>
       </span>
       ) is a city and
       <a href="/wiki/Municipality" title="Municipality">
        municipality
       </a>
       located in central Spain; it is the
       <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">
        capital
       </a>
       of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Province_(Spain)" title="Province (Spain)">
        province
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Toledo" title="Province of Toledo">
        Toledo
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_communities_of_Spain" title="Autonomous communities of Spain">
        autonomous community
       </a>
       of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Castile%E2%80%93La_Mancha" title="Castile–La Mancha">
        Castile–La Mancha
       </a>
       . Toledo was declared a
       <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">
        World Heritage Site
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">
        UNESCO
       </a>
       in 1986 for its extensive monumental and cultural heritage.
      </p>
      <p>
       Toledo is known as the "Imperial City" for having been the main venue of the court of
       <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">
        Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor
       </a>
       , and as the "City of the Three Cultures" for the cultural influences of Christians, Muslims and Jews reflected in its history. It was also the capital of the ancient Visigothic kingdom of Hispania, which followed the fall of the Roman Empire, and the location of historic events such as the
       <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">
        Visigothic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Councils_of_Toledo" title="Councils of Toledo">
        Councils of Toledo
       </a>
       . Toledo has a long history in the production of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bladed_weapon" title="Bladed weapon">
        bladed weapons
       </a>
       , which are now popular souvenirs of the city.
      </p>
      <p>
       People who were born or have lived in Toledo include
       <a href="/wiki/Brunhilda_of_Austrasia" title="Brunhilda of Austrasia">
        Brunhilda of Austrasia
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Ish%C4%81q_Ibr%C4%81h%C4%ABm_al-Zarq%C4%81l%C4%AB" title="Abū Ishāq Ibrāhīm al-Zarqālī">
        Al-Zarqali
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Garcilaso_de_la_Vega_(poet)" title="Garcilaso de la Vega (poet)">
        Garcilaso de la Vega
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_of_Toledo" title="Eleanor of Toledo">
        Eleanor of Toledo
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alfonso_X" title="Alfonso X">
        Alfonso X
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/El_Greco" title="El Greco">
        El Greco
       </a>
       . As of 2015
       <sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Toledo,_Spain&amp;action=edit">
         [update]
        </a>
       </sup>
       , the city had a population of 83,226.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and an area of 232.1 km
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       (89.6 sq mi).
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Coat_of_arms">
        Coat of arms
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Toledo,_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Coat of arms">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Toledo_(Spain)" title="Coat of arms of Toledo (Spain)">
        Coat of arms of Toledo (Spain)
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The town was granted arms in the 16th century, which by special royal privilege was based on the
       <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_Spain" title="Coat of arms of Spain">
        royal of arms of Spain
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Toledo,_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/History_of_Toledo,_Spain" title="History of Toledo, Spain">
        History of Toledo, Spain
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Toledo,_Spain" title="Timeline of Toledo, Spain">
        Timeline of Toledo, Spain
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity">
        Antiquity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Toledo,_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Antiquity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Toledo (
       <small>
        Latin:
       </small>
       <i>
        Toletum
       </i>
       ) is mentioned by the Roman historian Livy (ca. 59 BCE – 17 CE) as
       <i>
        urbs parva, sed loco munita
       </i>
       ("a small city, but fortified by location"). Roman general
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marcus_Fulvius_Nobilior" title="Marcus Fulvius Nobilior">
        Marcus Fulvius Nobilior
       </a>
       fought a battle near the city in 193 BCE against a confederation of
       <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">
        Celtic
       </a>
       tribes including the
       <a href="/wiki/Vaccaei" title="Vaccaei">
        Vaccaei
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Vettones" title="Vettones">
        Vettones
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Celtiberians" title="Celtiberians">
        Celtiberi
       </a>
       , defeating them and capturing a king called Hilermus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At that time, Toletum was a city of the
       <a href="/wiki/Carpetani" title="Carpetani">
        Carpetani
       </a>
       tribe, and part of the region of
       <a href="/wiki/Carpetania" title="Carpetania">
        Carpetania
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was incorporated into the Roman Empire as a
       <i>
        civitas stipendiaria
       </i>
       , that is, a tributary city of non-citizens, and by
       <a href="/wiki/Flavian_dynasty" title="Flavian dynasty">
        Flavian
       </a>
       times it had achieved the status of
       <a href="/wiki/Municipium" title="Municipium">
        municipium
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       With this status, city officials, even of Carpetani origin, obtained Roman citizenship for public service, and the forms of Roman law and politics were increasingly adopted.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At approximately this time were constructed in Toletum a
       <a href="/wiki/Circus_(building)" title="Circus (building)">
        Roman circus
       </a>
       , city walls,
       <a href="/wiki/Thermae" title="Thermae">
        public baths
       </a>
       , and a municipal water supply and storage system.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Roman circus in Toledo was one of the largest in Hispania, at 423 metres (1,388 feet) long and 100 metres (330 feet) wide, with a track dimension of 408 metres (1,339 feet) long and 86 metres (282 feet) wide.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Chariot races were held on special holidays and were also commissioned by private citizens to celebrate career achievements. A fragmentary stone inscription records circus games paid for by a citizen of unknown name to celebrate his achieving the sevirate, a kind of priesthood conferring high status. Archaeologists have also identified portions of a special seat of the sort used by the city elites to attend circus games, called a
       <i>
        sella curulis
       </i>
       . The circus could hold up to 15000 spectators.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scribd.com_7-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-scribd.com-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During Roman times, Toledo was never a provincial capital nor a
       <a href="/wiki/Conventus_iuridicus" title="Conventus iuridicus">
        conventus iuridicus
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Late_Roman_Spain_and_Its_Cities_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Late_Roman_Spain_and_Its_Cities-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       but it started to gain importance in late antiquity. There are indications that large private houses (
       <i>
        domus
       </i>
       ) within the city walls were enlarged, while several large villas were built north of the city through the third and fourth centuries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Games were held in the circus into the late fourth and early fifth centuries C.E., also an indication of active city life and ongoing patronage by wealthy elites.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
        <a href="#cite_note-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A church council was held in Toledo in the year 400 to discuss the conflict with
       <a href="/wiki/Priscillianism" title="Priscillianism">
        Priscillianism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A second council of Toledo was held in 527. The
       <a href="/wiki/Visigoths" title="Visigoths">
        Visigothic
       </a>
       king
       <a href="/wiki/Theudis" title="Theudis">
        Theudis
       </a>
       was in Toledo in 546, where he promulgated a law. This is strong though not certain evidence that Toledo was the chief residence for Theudis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Late_Roman_Spain_and_Its_Cities_8-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Late_Roman_Spain_and_Its_Cities-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       King
       <a href="/wiki/Athanagild" title="Athanagild">
        Athanagild
       </a>
       died in Toledo, probably in 568. Although Theudis and Athangild based themselves in Toledo, Toledo was not yet the capital city of the Iberian peninsula, as Theudis and Athangild's power was limited in extent, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suevi" title="Suevi">
        Suevi
       </a>
       ruling
       <a href="/wiki/Gallaecia" title="Gallaecia">
        Galicia
       </a>
       and local elites dominating
       <a href="/wiki/Lusitania" title="Lusitania">
        Lusitania
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Hispania_Baetica" title="Hispania Baetica">
        Betica
       </a>
       , and Cantabria.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This changed with
       <a href="/wiki/Liuvigild" title="Liuvigild">
        Liuvigild
       </a>
       (Leovigild), who brought the peninsula under his control. The Visigoths ruled from Toledo until the
       <a href="/wiki/Moors" title="Moors">
        Moors
       </a>
       conquered the Iberian peninsula in the early years of 8th century (711–719).
      </p>
      <p>
       Today the historic center contains basements, passages, wells, baths and ancient water pipes that since Roman times have been used in the city.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Visigothic_Toledo">
        Visigothic Toledo
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Toledo,_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Visigothic Toledo">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Civitas_Toletana,_AD_976.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="463" data-file-width="686" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Civitas_Toletana%2C_AD_976.jpg/200px-Civitas_Toletana%2C_AD_976.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Civitas_Toletana%2C_AD_976.jpg/300px-Civitas_Toletana%2C_AD_976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Civitas_Toletana%2C_AD_976.jpg/400px-Civitas_Toletana%2C_AD_976.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Civitas_Toletana,_AD_976.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         The city of Toledo as depicted in the
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Codex_Vigilanus" title="Codex Vigilanus">
           Codex Vigilanus
          </a>
         </i>
         in 976.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A series of
       <a href="/wiki/Councils_of_Toledo" title="Councils of Toledo">
        church councils
       </a>
       was held in Toledo under the Visigoths. A synod of Arian bishops was held in 580 to discuss theological reconciliation with Nicene Christianity.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Liuvigild's successor,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reccared" title="Reccared">
        Reccared
       </a>
       , hosted the
       <a href="/wiki/Third_Council_of_Toledo" title="Third Council of Toledo">
        third council of Toledo
       </a>
       , at which the Visigothic kings abandoned
       <a href="/wiki/Arianism" title="Arianism">
        Arianism
       </a>
       and reconciled with the existing Hispano-Roman episcopate.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A synod held in 610 transferred the metropolitanate of the old province of Carthaginensis from Cartagena to Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
        <a href="#cite_note-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At that time, Cartagena was ruled by the Byzantines, and this move ensured a closer relation between the bishops of Spain and the Visigothic kings. King
       <a href="/wiki/Sisebut" title="Sisebut">
        Sisebut
       </a>
       forced Jews in the Visigothic kingdom to convert to Christianity; this act was criticized and efforts were made to reverse it at the
       <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Fourth Council of Toledo">
        Fourth Council of Toledo
       </a>
       in 633.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Fifth Council of Toledo">
        Fifth
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Sixth Council of Toledo">
        Sixth
       </a>
       Councils of Toledo placed church sanctions on anyone who would challenge the Visigothic kings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Seventh_Council_of_Toledo" title="Seventh Council of Toledo">
        Seventh Council of Toledo
       </a>
       instituted a requirement that all bishops in the area of a royal city, that is, of Toledo, must reside for one month per year in Toledo. This was a stage in "the elevation of Toledo as the primatial see of the whole church of the Visgothic kingdom".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In addition, the seventh council declared that any clergy fleeing the kingdom, assisting conspirators against the king, or aiding conspirators, would be
       <a href="/wiki/Excommunication" title="Excommunication">
        excommunicated
       </a>
       and no one should remove this sentence. The ban on lifing these sentences of excommunication was lifted at the
       <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Eighth Council of Toledo">
        Eighth Council of Toledo
       </a>
       in 653, at which, for the first time, decisions were signed by palace officials as well as bishops.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The eighth council of Toledo took measures that enhanced Toledo's significance as the center of royal power in the Iberian peninsula. The council declared that the election of a new king following the death of the old one should only take place in the royal city, or wherever the old king died.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Visigothic_Spain,_409-711_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Visigothic_Spain,_409-711-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In practice this handed the power to choose kings to only such palace officials and military commanders who were in regular attendance on the king. The decision also took king-making power away from the bishops, who would be in their own sees and would not have time to come together to attend the royal election. The decision did allow the bishop of Toledo, alone among bishops, to be involved in decisions concerning the royal Visigothic succession. The
       <a href="/wiki/Ninth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Ninth Council of Toledo">
        ninth
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Tenth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Tenth Council of Toledo">
        tenth
       </a>
       councils were held in rapid succession in 655 and 656.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Visigothic_Spain,_409-711_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Visigothic_Spain,_409-711-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reccesuinth" title="Reccesuinth">
        Reccesuinth
       </a>
       died in 672 at his villa in Gerticos, his successor
       <a href="/wiki/Wamba_(king)" title="Wamba (king)">
        Wamba
       </a>
       was elected on the spot, then went to Toledo to be anointed king by the bishop of Toledo, according to the procedures laid out in prior church councils.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 673, Wamba defeated a rebel duke named Paul, and held his victory parade in Toledo. The parade included ritual humiliation and scalping of the defeated Paul.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Wamba carried out renovation works in Toledo in 674-675, marking these with inscriptions above the city gates that are no longer extant but were recorded in the eighth century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Eleventh_Council_of_Toledo" title="Eleventh Council of Toledo">
        Eleventh Council of Toledo
       </a>
       was held in 675 under king Wamba. Wamba weakened the power of the bishop of Toledo by creating a new bishopric outside Toledo at the church of Saints Peter and Paul. This was one of the main churches of Toledo and was the church where Wamba was anointed king, and the church from which Visigothic kings departed for war after special ceremonies in which they were presented with a relic of the
       <a href="/wiki/True_Cross" title="True Cross">
        True Cross
       </a>
       . By creating a new bishopric there, Wamba removed power over royal succession from the bishop of Toledo and granted it to the new bishop.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Twelfth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Twelfth Council of Toledo">
        Twelfth Council of Toledo
       </a>
       was held in 681 after Wamba's removal from office. Convinced that he was dying, Wamba had accepted a state of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penitence" title="Penitence">
        penitence
       </a>
       that according to the decision of a previous church council, made him inelegible to remain king. The Twelfth Council, led by newly installed bishop
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_of_Toledo" title="Julian of Toledo">
        Julian
       </a>
       confirmed the validity of Wamba's removal from office and his succession by
       <a href="/wiki/Erwig" title="Erwig">
        Ervig
       </a>
       . The Twelfth Council eliminated the new bishopric that Wamba had created and returned the powers over succession to the bishop of Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The Twelfth Council of Toledo approved 28 laws against the Jews. Julian of Toledo, despite a Jewish origin, was strongly anti-Semitic as reflected in his writings and activities.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The leading Jews of Toledo were assembled in the church of Saint Mary on January 27, 681, where the new laws were read out to them.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Thirteenth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Thirteenth Council of Toledo">
        Thirteenth
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Fourteenth Council of Toledo">
        Fourteenth
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Fifteenth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Fifteenth Council of Toledo">
        Fifteenth
       </a>
       Councils of Toledo were held in 683, 684, and 688. The Thirteenth Council restored property and legal rights to those who had rebelled against King Wamba in 673.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Thirteenth Council also approved laws protecting the king's family after the king's death. In 687, Ervig took the penitent state before dying, and the kingship passed to
       <a href="/wiki/Egica" title="Egica">
        Egica
       </a>
       , who was anointed king in Toledo on November 24.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 688, the Fifteenth Council lifted the ban on taking property from the families of former kings, whereupon Egica was able to plunder Ervig's family properties.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In the late seventh century, Toledo became a main center of literacy and writing in the Iberian peninsula. Toledo's development as a center of learning was influenced by
       <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">
        Isidore of Seville
       </a>
       , an author and advocate of literacy who attended several church councils in Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
        <a href="#cite_note-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       King
       <a href="/wiki/Chindasuinth" title="Chindasuinth">
        Chindasuinth
       </a>
       had a royal library in Toledo, and at least one count called Laurentius had a private library.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Sometime before 651, Chindasuinth sent the bishop of Zaragoza,
       <a href="/wiki/Taius" title="Taius">
        Taio
       </a>
       , to Rome to obtain books that were not available in Toledo. Taio obtained, at least, parts of pope Gregory's
       <a href="/wiki/Commentary_on_Job" title="Commentary on Job">
        <i>
         Moralia
        </i>
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The library also contained a copy of a
       <a href="/wiki/Hexameron" title="Hexameron">
        Hexameron
       </a>
       by
       <a href="/wiki/Dracontius" title="Dracontius">
        Dracontius
       </a>
       , which Chindasuinth liked so much that he commissioned
       <a href="/wiki/Eugenius_II_of_Toledo" title="Eugenius II of Toledo">
        Eugenius II
       </a>
       to revise it by adding a new part dealing with the seventh day of creation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Chindasuinth issued laws that were gathered together in a book called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liber_Iudiciorum" title="Liber Iudiciorum">
        Liber Iudiciorum
       </a>
       by his successor Reccesuinth in 654; this book was revised twice, widely copied, and was an important influence on medieval Spanish law.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Three bishops of Toledo wrote works that were widely copied and disseminated in western Europe and parts of which survive to this day:
       <a href="/wiki/Eugenius_II_of_Toledo" title="Eugenius II of Toledo">
        Eugenius II
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ildefonsus#Theology_and_Writings" title="Ildefonsus">
        Ildefonsus
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_of_Toledo" title="Julian of Toledo">
        Julian
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
       "In intellectual terms the leading Spanish churchmen of the seventh century had no equals before the appearance of Bede."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arab_Conquest_of_Spain_37-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arab_Conquest_of_Spain-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 693, the
       <a href="/wiki/Sixteenth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Sixteenth Council of Toledo">
        Sixteenth Council of Toledo
       </a>
       condemned
       <a href="/wiki/Sisebert" title="Sisebert">
        Sisebert
       </a>
       , Julian's successor as bishop of Toledo, for having rebelled against King Egica in alliance with Liuvigoto, the widow of king Ervig.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A rebel king called
       <a href="/wiki/Suniefred" title="Suniefred">
        Suniefred
       </a>
       seized power in Toledo briefly at about this time. Whether or not Sisebert's and Suniefred's rebellions were the same or separate is unknown. Suniefred is known only from having minted coins in Toledo during what should have been Egica's reign.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
        <a href="#cite_note-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Seventeenth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Seventeenth Council of Toledo">
        Seventeenth Council of Toledo
       </a>
       was held in 694. The Eighteenth Council of Toledo, the last one, took place shortly after Egica's death around 702 or 703.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By the end of the seventh century the bishop of Toledo was the leader of the Spanish bishops, a situation unusual in Europe: "The metropolitan bishops of Toledo had achieved by the last quarter of the seventh century an authority and a primacy that was unique in Western Europe. Not even the pope could count on such support from neighbouring metropolitans."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arab_Conquest_of_Spain_37-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Arab_Conquest_of_Spain-37">
         [37]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Toledo "had been matched by no other city in western Europe outside Italy as the governmental and symbolic center of a powerful monarchy".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Toledo had "emerged from relative obscurity to become the permanent governmental centre of the Visigothic monarchy; a true capital, whose only equivalent in western Europe was to be Lombard Pavia".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       When
       <a href="/wiki/Wittiza" title="Wittiza">
        Wittiza
       </a>
       died around 710,
       <a href="/wiki/Roderic" title="Roderic">
        Ruderic
       </a>
       became Visigothic king in Toledo, but the kingdom was split, as a rival king
       <a href="/wiki/Achila_II" title="Achila II">
        Achila
       </a>
       ruled Tarraconensis and Narbonensis.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Meanwhile, Arabic and Berber troops under
       <a href="/wiki/Musa_bin_Nusayr" title="Musa bin Nusayr">
        Musa ibn Nusayr
       </a>
       had conquered Tangiers and Ceuta between 705 and 710, and commenced raids into the Visigothic kingdom in 711.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
        <a href="#cite_note-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Ruderic led an army to confront the raiders. He was defeated and killed in battle, apparently after being betrayed by Visigothic nobles who wished to replace him as king and did not consider the Arabs and Berbers a serious threat. The commander of the invading forces was
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tariq_bin_Ziyad" title="Tariq bin Ziyad">
        Tariq bin Ziyad
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Libu" title="Libu">
        Luwata
       </a>
       Berber freedman in the service of governor Musa.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It is possible that a king called
       <a href="/wiki/Oppas" title="Oppas">
        Oppa
       </a>
       ruled in Toledo between Ruderic's death and the fall of Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Tariq, seizing the opportunity presented by the death of Ruderic and the internal divisions of the Visigothic nobles, captured Toledo, in 711 or 712.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Governor Musa disembarked in Cádiz and proceeded to Toledo, where he executed numerous Visigothic nobles, thus destroying much of the Visigothic power structure.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
        <a href="#cite_note-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Collins suggests that the Visigothic emphasis on Toledo as the center of royal ceremony became a weakness. Since the king was chosen in or around Toledo, by nobles based in Toledo, and had to be anointed king by the bishop of Toledo in a church in Toledo, when Tariq captured Toledo and executed the Visigothic nobles, having already killed the king, there was no way for the Visigoths to select a legitimate king.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
        <a href="#cite_note-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
        <a href="#cite_note-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Toledo_under_Arab_rule">
        Toledo under Arab rule
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Toledo,_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Toledo under Arab rule">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Soon after the conquest, Musa and Tariq returned to Damascus. The Arab center of administration was placed first in Seville, then moved to Cordoba. With most of the rest of the Iberian peninsula, Toledo was ruled from Cordoba by the
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_Umayyad_governors_of_al-Andalus" title="List of Umayyad governors of al-Andalus">
        governor
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">
        Al-Andalus
       </a>
       , under the ultimate notional command of the
       <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">
        Umayyad Caliph
       </a>
       in Damascus. Arab conquerors had often replaced former capital cities with new ones to mark the change in political power, and they did so here: "Toledo suffered a period of profound decline throughout much of the earlier centuries of Arab dominance in the peninsula."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The invaders were ethnically diverse, and available evidence suggests that in the area of Toledo, Berber settlement predominated over Arab.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 742 the Berbers in Al-Andalus rebelled against the Arab Omeyyad governors. They took control of the north and marched south, laying siege to Toledo. After a siege of one month the Berber troops were defeated outside Toledo by troops sent from Cordoba by the governor
       <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Malik_ibn_Katan_al-Fihri" title="Abd al-Malik ibn Katan al-Fihri">
        Abd al-Malik ibn Katan
       </a>
       and commanded by the governor's son.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, while Ibn Katan's troops were engaged with the Berbers, his Arab allies betrayed and killed him and took over Cordoba. After the Arabs' first leader,
       <a href="/wiki/Thalaba_ibn_Salama_al-Amili" title="Thalaba ibn Salama al-Amili">
        Talama ibn Salama
       </a>
       , died,
       <a href="/wiki/Yusuf_ibn_%27Abd_al-Rahman_al-Fihri" title="Yusuf ibn 'Abd al-Rahman al-Fihri">
        Yusuf al-Fihri
       </a>
       became ruler of Al-Andalus. The Omeyyad dynasty in Damascus collapsed and Yusuf ruled independently with the support of his Syrian Arab forces. The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qais" title="Qais">
        Qays
       </a>
       Arab commander As-Sumayl was made governor of Toledo under Yusuf around 753.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is evidence that Toledo retained its importance as a literary and ecclesiastical center into the middle 700s, in the
       <a href="/wiki/Chronicle_of_754" title="Chronicle of 754">
        Chronicle of 754
       </a>
       , the life of Saint Ildefonsus by
       <a href="/wiki/Cixila" title="Cixila">
        Cixila
       </a>
       , and ecclesiastical letters sent from Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The eighth century bishop of Toledo,
       <a href="/wiki/Cixila" title="Cixila">
        Cixila
       </a>
       , wrote a life of
       <a href="/wiki/Ildefonsus" title="Ildefonsus">
        Saint Ildefonsus of Toledo
       </a>
       , probably before 737.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
        <a href="#cite_note-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This life of Ildefonsus emphasized two episodes in the life of the bishop of Toledo. In the first episode the covering of the tomb of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Leocadia" title="Saint Leocadia">
        Saint Leocadia
       </a>
       levitated while Ildefonsus was saying mass, with king
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reccesuinth" title="Reccesuinth">
        Reccesuinth
       </a>
       present. In the second episode
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">
        Mary
       </a>
       appears to Ildefonsus and Reccesuinth. These episodes are said to have resulted from Ildefonsus' devotion to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Leocadia" title="Saint Leocadia">
        Saint Leocadia
       </a>
       , patroness saint of Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Collins suggests that Cixila's life of Ildefonsus helped maintain Ildefonsus' appeal and helped the church in Toledo to retain some of its authority among Christian churches in the Iberian peninsula.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       An archdeacon in Toledo called Evantius, who was active around 720 and died in 737, wrote a letter to address the existence of judaizing tendencies among the Christians of Zaragoza, specifically the belief that there are unclean forms of meat and the literal interpretation of Deuteronomic law.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
        <a href="#cite_note-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A deacon and cantor from Toledo called Peter wrote a second letter, to Seville, in about the year 750, to explain that they were celebrating Easter and a September liturgical fast incorrectly, again confusing them with Jewish feasts celebrated at the same time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
        <a href="#cite_note-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These letters show that some of the primacy of the church of Toledo within the Iberian peninsula still existed in the 700s: "Not only were its clerics still well enough equipped in intellectual terms to provide authoritative guidance on a wide range of ecclesiastical discipline and doctrine, but this was also actively sought."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
        <a href="#cite_note-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       There is a strong possibility that the
       <a href="/wiki/Chronicle_of_754" title="Chronicle of 754">
        Chronicle of 754
       </a>
       was written in Toledo (though scholars have also proposed Cordoba and Guadix) based on the information available to the chronicler.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
        <a href="#cite_note-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The chronicler showed awareness of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historia_Gothorum" title="Historia Gothorum">
        Historia Gothorum
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Etymologiae" title="Etymologiae">
        Etymologiae
       </a>
       , and the chronicle of
       <a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">
        Isidore of Seville
       </a>
       , the work of
       <a href="/wiki/Braulio_of_Zaragoza" title="Braulio of Zaragoza">
        Braulio of Zaragoza
       </a>
       , the acts of the
       <a href="/wiki/Councils_of_Toledo" title="Councils of Toledo">
        councils of Toledo
       </a>
       , De Perpetua Virginitate by
       <a href="/wiki/Ildefonsus" title="Ildefonsus">
        Ildefonsus
       </a>
       , and De Comprobatione Sextae Aetatis and Historia Wambae by
       <a href="/wiki/Julian_of_Toledo" title="Julian of Toledo">
        Julian of Toledo
       </a>
       , all works that would have existed in the Visigothic libraries of seventh century Toledo and whose existence together "makes more sense in a Toledan context than in any other".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
        <a href="#cite_note-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 756
       <a href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_I" title="Abd al-Rahman I">
        Abd ar-Rahman
       </a>
       , a descendant of the fallen Omeyyad caliphs, came to Al-Andalus and initiated a revolt against Yusuf. He defeated Yusuf and forced him to reside in Cordoba, but Yusuf broke the agreement and raised a Berber army to fight Abd ar-Rahman. In this conflict, Toledo was held against Abd ar-Rahman by Yusuf's cousin Hisham ibn Urwa. Yusuf attempted to march on Seville, but was defeated and instead attempted to reach his cousin in Toledo. He was either killed on his way to Toledo, or he reached Toledo and held out there for as many as two or three years before being betrayed and killed by his own people. Whether or not Yusuf himself held out in Toledo, Hisham ibn Urwa did hold power in Toledo for several years, resisting the authority of Abd ar-Rahman. In 761 Hisham is reported as again being in rebellion in Toledo against Abd ar-Rahman. Abd ar-Rahman failed to take Toledo by force and instead signed a treaty allowing Hisham to remain in control of Toledo, but giving one of his sons as hostage to Abd ar-Rahman. Hisham continued to defy Abd ar-Rahman, who had Hisham's son executed and the head catapulted over the city walls into Toledo. Abd ar-Rahman attacked Toledo in 764, winning only when some of Hisham's own people betrayed him and turned him over to Abd ar-Rahman and his freedman Badr.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_al-Athir" title="Ali ibn al-Athir">
        Ibn al-Athir
       </a>
       states that towards the end of Abd ar-Rahman's reign, a governor of Toledo raided in force into the
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Asturias" title="Kingdom of Asturias">
        Kingdom of Asturias
       </a>
       during the reign of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauregatus" title="Mauregatus">
        Mauregatus
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
       though the Asturian chronicles do not record the event.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
        <a href="#cite_note-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Under the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Umayyad" title="Umayyad">
        Umayyad
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Cordoba" title="Emirate of Cordoba">
        Emirate of Cordoba
       </a>
       , Toledo was the centre of numerous insurrections dating from 761 to 857.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Twenty years after the rebellion of Hisham ibn Urwa, the last of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yusuf_al-Fihri" title="Yusuf al-Fihri">
        Yusuf
       </a>
       's sons, Abu al-Aswad ibn Yusuf, rebelled in Toledo in 785.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
        <a href="#cite_note-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       After the suppression of ibn Yusuf's revolt, Abd ar-Rahman's oldest son Sulayman was made governor of Toledo. However, Abd ar-Rahman designated as his successor a younger son,
       <a href="/wiki/Hisham_I_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Hisham I of Córdoba">
        Hisham
       </a>
       . On Hisham's accession to the Emirate in 788, Sulayman refused to make the oath of allegiance at the mosque, as succession custom would have dictated, and thus declared himself in rebellion. He was joined in Toledo by his brother Abdallah. Hisham laid siege to Toledo. While Abdallah held Toledo against Hisham, Sulayman escaped and attempted to find support elsewhere, but was unsuccessful. In 789, Abdallah submitted and Hisham took control of Toledo. The following year, Sulayman gave up the fight and went into exile.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
        <a href="#cite_note-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Hisham's son
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Hakam_I" title="Al-Hakam I">
        Al-Hakam
       </a>
       was governor of Toledo from 792 to 796 when he succeeded his father as emir in Cordoba.
      </p>
      <p>
       After Al-Hakam's accession and departure, a poet resident in Toledo named Girbib ibn-Abdallah wrote verses against the Omeyyads, helping to inspire a revolt in Toledo against the new emir in 797. Chroniclers disagree as to the leader of this revolt, though Ibn Hayyan states that it was led by Ibn Hamir. Al-Hakam sent
       <a href="/wiki/Amrus_ibn_Yusuf" title="Amrus ibn Yusuf">
        Amrus ibn Yusuf
       </a>
       to fight the rebellion. Amrus took control of the Berber troops in
       <a href="/wiki/Talavera_de_la_Reina" title="Talavera de la Reina">
        Talavera
       </a>
       . From there, Amrus negotiated with a faction inside Toledo called the Banu Mahsa, promising to make them governors if they would betray Ibn Hamir. The Banu Mahsa brought Ibn Hamir's head to Amrus at Talavera, but instead of making them governors, Amrus executed them. Amrus now persuaded the remaining factions in Toledo to submit to him. Once he entered Toledo, he invited the leaders to a celebratory feast. As they entered Amrus' fortress, the guests were beheaded one by one and their bodies thrown in a specially dug ditch. The massacre was thus called "The Day of the Ditch". Amrus' soldiers killed about 700 people that day. Amrus was governor of Toledo until 802.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
        <a href="#cite_note-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       "In 785, Bishop Elipandus of Toledo wrote a letter condemning the teaching of a certain Migetius."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In his letter,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elipandus" title="Elipandus">
        Elipandus
       </a>
       asserted that Christ had adopted his humanity, a position that came to be known as
       <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Adoptionism" title="Spanish Adoptionism">
        Adoptionism
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Two Asturian bishops,
       <a href="/wiki/Beatus_of_Li%C3%A9bana" title="Beatus of Liébana">
        Beatus
       </a>
       and Eterius, bishop of Osma, wrote a treatise condemning Elipandus' views.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Pope Hadrian wrote a letter between 785 and 791 in which he condemned Migetius, but also the terminology used by Elipandus.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Frankish court of Charlemagne also condemned Adoptionism at the Synod of Frankfurt in 794.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Although Ramon Abadals y de Vinyals argued that this controversy represented an ideological assertion of independence by the Asturian church against the Moslem-ruled church of Toledo,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Collins believes this argument applies eleventh century ideology to the eighth century and is anachronistic.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
        <a href="#cite_note-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, Collins notes that the controversy and the alliances formed during it between Asturias and the Franks broke the old unity of the Spanish church.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
        <a href="#cite_note-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The influence of the bishops of Toledo would be much more limited until the eleventh century.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
        <a href="#cite_note-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By the end of the 700s, the Omeyyads had created three frontier districts stretching out from the southern core of their Iberian territories. These were called the
       <a href="/wiki/Lower_March" title="Lower March">
        Lower March
       </a>
       (al-Tagr al-Adna), Central March (al-Tagr al-Awsat), and
       <a href="/wiki/Upper_March" title="Upper March">
        Upper March
       </a>
       (al-Tagr al-A'la). Toledo became the administrative center of the Central March, while
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merida,_Spain" title="Merida, Spain">
        Merida
       </a>
       became the center of the Lower March and
       <a href="/wiki/Zaragoza" title="Zaragoza">
        Zaragoza
       </a>
       of the Upper March.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Following the death of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_II" title="Abd al-Rahman II">
        Abd al-Rahman II
       </a>
       , a new revolt broke out in Toledo. The Omeyyad governor was held hostage in order to secure the return of Toledan hostages held in Córdoba. Toledo now engaged in an inter-city feud with the nearby city of
       <a href="/wiki/Calatrava_la_Vieja" title="Calatrava la Vieja">
        Calatrava la Vieja
       </a>
       . Toledan soldiers attacked Calatrava, destroyed the walls, and massacred or expelled many inhabitants of Calatrava in 853. Soldiers from Cordoba came to restore the walls and protect Calatrava from Toledo. The new emir,
       <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_I_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Muhammad I of Córdoba">
        Muhammad I
       </a>
       , sent a second army to attack the Toledans, but was defeated. Toledo now made an alliance with King
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordo%C3%B1o_I" title="Ordoño I">
        Ordoño I
       </a>
       of Asturias. The Toledans and Asturians were defeated at the Battle of Guadacelete, with sources claiming 8000 Toledan and Asturian soldiers were killed and their heads sent back to Cordoba for display throughout Al-Andalus. Despite this defeat, Toledo did not surrender to Cordoba. The Omeyyads reinforced nearby fortresses with cavalry forces to try to contain the Toledans. Toledans attacked
       <a href="/wiki/Talavera_de_la_Reina" title="Talavera de la Reina">
        Talavera
       </a>
       in 857, but were again defeated. In 858 emir
       <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_I_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Muhammad I of Córdoba">
        Muhammad I
       </a>
       personally led an expedition against Toledo and destroyed a bridge, but was unable to take the city. In 859, Muhammad I negotiated a truce with Toledo. Toledo became virtually independent for twenty years, though locked in conflict with neighboring cities. Muhammad I recovered control of Toledo in 873, when he successfully besieged the city and forced it to submit.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
        <a href="#cite_note-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alfonso_VI_reconquista_Toledo.JPG">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4608" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Alfonso_VI_reconquista_Toledo.JPG/220px-Alfonso_VI_reconquista_Toledo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Alfonso_VI_reconquista_Toledo.JPG/330px-Alfonso_VI_reconquista_Toledo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Alfonso_VI_reconquista_Toledo.JPG/440px-Alfonso_VI_reconquista_Toledo.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alfonso_VI_reconquista_Toledo.JPG" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Azulejo in Seville's
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plaza_de_Espa%C3%B1a_(Seville)" title="Plaza de España (Seville)">
          Plaza de España
         </a>
         depicting the conquest of Toledo in 1085.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Banu_Qasi" title="Banu Qasi">
        Banu Qasi
       </a>
       gained nominal control of the city until 920 and in 932
       <a href="/wiki/Abd-ar-Rahman_III" title="Abd-ar-Rahman III">
        Abd-ar-Rahman III
       </a>
       captured the city following an extensive siege.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       According to the
       <a href="/wiki/Chronicle_of_Alfonso_III" title="Chronicle of Alfonso III">
        Chronicle of Alfonso III
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musa_ibn_Musa" title="Musa ibn Musa">
        Musa ibn Musa
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Banu_Qasi" title="Banu Qasi">
        Banu Qasi
       </a>
       had, partly by war and partly by strategy, made himself master of Zaragoza, Tudela, Huesca, and Toledo. He had installed his son Lupus (Lubb) as governor of Toledo. King Ordoño I of Asturias fought a series of battles with Musa ibn Musa. According to the Chronicle, Musa ibn Musa allied with his brother-in-law Garcia, identified as Garcia Iñiquez, King of Pamplona. Ordoño defeated Musa's forces at the
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Monte_Laturce" title="Battle of Monte Laturce">
        Battle of Monte Laturce
       </a>
       . Musa died of injuries, and his son Lubb submitted to Ordoño's authority in 862 or 863, for the duration of Ordoño's reign (up to 866). Thus, according to the Chronicle of Alfonso III, Toledo was ruled by the Asturian kings. However, Arabic sources do not confirm these campaigns, instead stating that Musa ibn Musa was killed in a failed attack on Guadalajara, and that Andalusi forces repeatedly defeated Asturian forces in the area of Alava from 862 to 866.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
        <a href="#cite_note-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       By the 870s the Omeyyads had regained control over Toledo. In 878
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Mundhir_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Al-Mundhir of Córdoba">
        Al-Mundhir
       </a>
       led an expedition against Asturias, of which one of the main components was a force from Toledo. One source portrays this raid as an attack by the 'King of Toledo', but other sources portray it as an Omeyyad raid involving substantial Toledan forces. The forces from Toledo were defeated by
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_III_of_Asturias" title="Alfonso III of Asturias">
        Alfonso III of Asturias
       </a>
       at the
       <a class="extiw" href="https://es.wikipedia.org/wiki/Batalla_de_Polvoraria" title="es:Batalla de Polvoraria">
        Battle of Polvoraria
       </a>
       . Spanish chronicles state that twelve to thirteen thousand in the Toledo army were killed in the battle. Collins states that these figures are "totally unreliable" but demonstrate that Asturian chroniclers thought of this as an important and decisive battle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
        <a href="#cite_note-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 920s and 930s, the governors of Toledo were in rebellion against the Umayyad regime in Cordoba, led by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abd_al-Rahman_III" title="Abd al-Rahman III">
        Abd al-Rahman III
       </a>
       . In 930, Abd al-Rahman III, having now adopted the title of
       <a href="/wiki/Caliphate_of_C%C3%B3rdoba" title="Caliphate of Córdoba">
        caliph
       </a>
       , attacked Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
        <a href="#cite_note-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The governor of Toledo asked for help from King
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ramiro_II_of_Leon" title="Ramiro II of Leon">
        Ramiro II of Leon
       </a>
       , but Ramiro was preocuppied with a civil war against his brother
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_IV_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Alfonso IV of León">
        Alfonso IV
       </a>
       and was unable to help.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
        <a href="#cite_note-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 932, Abd al-Rahman III conquered Toledo, re-establishing control of al-Tagr al-Awsat, the Central March of the Omeyyad state.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
        <a href="#cite_note-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1009 one of the last Umayyad caliphs,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_II_al-Mahdi" title="Muhammad II al-Mahdi">
        Muhammad II al-Mahdi
       </a>
       , fled to Toledo after being expelled from Cordoba by Berber forces backing the rival claimant
       <a href="/wiki/Sulayman_ibn_al-Hakam" title="Sulayman ibn al-Hakam">
        Sulayman
       </a>
       . Al-Mahdi and his
       <a href="/wiki/Saqaliba" title="Saqaliba">
        Saqaliba
       </a>
       general Wadih formed an alliance with the Count of Barcelona and his brother the Count of Urgell. These Catalans joined with Wadih and al-Mahdi in Toledo in 1010 and marched on Cordoba. The combination of Wadih's army and the Catalans defeated the Berbers in a
       <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Aqbat_al-Bakr" title="Battle of Aqbat al-Bakr">
        battle outside Cordoba
       </a>
       in 1010.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
        <a href="#cite_note-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After the fall of the Omeyyad caliphate in the early 11th century, Toledo became an independent
       <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">
        taifa
       </a>
       kingdom. The population of Toledo at this time was about 28 thousand, including a Jewish population estimated at 4 thousand.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
        <a href="#cite_note-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The Mozarab community had its own Christian bishop, and after the Christian conquest of Toledo, the city was a destination for Mozarab immigration from the Muslim south.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The taifa of Toledo was centered on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tajo_River" title="Tajo River">
        Tajo River
       </a>
       . The border with the taifa of Badajoz was on the Tajo between Talavera de la Reina and Coria. North, the border was the
       <a href="/wiki/Sierra_de_Guadarrama" title="Sierra de Guadarrama">
        Sierra de Guadarrama
       </a>
       . Northeast, Toledo lands stretched past
       <a href="/wiki/Guadalajara,_Castilla-La_Mancha" title="Guadalajara, Castilla-La Mancha">
        Guadalajara
       </a>
       to
       <a href="/wiki/Medinaceli" title="Medinaceli">
        Medinaceli
       </a>
       . Southeast was the border with the taifa of Valencia, in La Mancha between Cuenca and Albacete. South were the borders with Badajoz around the
       <a href="/wiki/Montes_de_Toledo" title="Montes de Toledo">
        Mountains of Toledo
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1062,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fernando_I_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Fernando I of León">
        Fernando I
       </a>
       of Leon and Castile attacked the taifa of Toledo. He conquered
       <a href="/wiki/Talamanca_de_Jarama" title="Talamanca de Jarama">
        Talamanca de Jarama
       </a>
       and besieged
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcala_de_Henares" title="Alcala de Henares">
        Alcala de Henares
       </a>
       . To secure Fernando's withdrawal, king
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Mamun_of_Toledo" title="Al-Mamun of Toledo">
        al-Mamun of Toledo
       </a>
       agreed to pay an annual tribute, or
       <a href="/wiki/Parias" title="Parias">
        parias
       </a>
       , to Fernando.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
        <a href="#cite_note-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Three years later in 1065, al-Mamun invaded the
       <a href="/wiki/Taifa_of_Valencia" title="Taifa of Valencia">
        taifa of Valencia
       </a>
       through La Mancha, successfully conquering it. Toledo controlled the taifa of Valencia until al-Mamun's death in 1075.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After the death of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fernando_I_of_Le%C3%B3n" title="Fernando I of León">
        Fernando I
       </a>
       in 1065, the kingdom of Leon and Castilla was divided in three: the kingdoms of Galicia, Leon, and Castilla. The parias that had been paid by Toledo to Fernando I were assigned to the Kingdom of León, which was inherited by Alfonso VI.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, in 1071, Alfonso's older brother
       <a href="/wiki/Sancho_II_of_Castile_and_Le%C3%B3n" title="Sancho II of Castile and León">
        Sancho II
       </a>
       invaded Leon and defeated his younger brother. Alfonso VI was allowed to go into exile with
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Mamun_of_Toledo" title="Al-Mamun of Toledo">
        al-Mamun
       </a>
       in Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Alfonso VI was in exile in Toledo approximately from June to October 1071, but after Sancho II was killed later in the same year, Alfonso left Toledo and returned to Leon. Some sources state that al-Mamun forced Alfonso to swear support for al-Mamun and his heirs before allowing him to leave.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1074,
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_VI_of_Le%C3%B3n_and_Castile" title="Alfonso VI of León and Castile">
        Alfonso VI
       </a>
       campaigned against the
       <a href="/wiki/Taifa_of_Granada" title="Taifa of Granada">
        taifa of Granada
       </a>
       with the assistance of
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Mamun_of_Toledo" title="Al-Mamun of Toledo">
        al-Mamun of Toledo
       </a>
       . Alfonso received troops from al-Mamun in addition to the parias payment, facilitating his military campaigns. The campaign was successful, and Granada was forced to begin
       <a href="/wiki/Parias" title="Parias">
        parias
       </a>
       payments to Alfonso VI. After this, al-Mamun proceeded to attack Cordoba, which was then under the control of his enemy
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27tamid_ibn_Abbad" title="Al-Mu'tamid ibn Abbad">
        al-Mutamid
       </a>
       , taifa king of Sevilla. He conquered Cordoba in January 1075.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Parias" title="Parias">
        parias
       </a>
       of Toledo to Alfonso VI in the 1070s amounted to approximately 12 thousand gold dinars. This money contributed strongly to Alfonso VI's ability to project military strength throughout the Iberian peninsula.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
        <a href="#cite_note-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       In 1076,
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Mamun_of_Toledo" title="Al-Mamun of Toledo">
        al-Mamun of Toledo
       </a>
       was killed in the city of Cordoba, which he had conquered only the year before. The taifa king of Sevilla took the opportunity to reconquer Cordoba and seize other territory on the borderlands between the taifas of Sevilla and Toledo. Al-Mamun was succeeded by his son, al-Qadir, the last taifa king of Toledo. Possibly keeping an earlier promise to al-Mamun, Alfonso VI at first supported the succession of al-Qadir. The taifa of Valencia, which had been conquered by al-Mamun, revolted against al-Qadir and ceased parias payments to Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Taking advantage of al-Qadir's weakness,
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Mu%27tamid_ibn_Abbad" title="Al-Mu'tamid ibn Abbad">
        al-Mutamid
       </a>
       of Sevilla took lands in La Mancha from the taifa of Toledo, and from there conquered the taifas of Valencia and Denia in 1078. After this, al-Qadir lost popularity in Toledo. There was a revolt against him, and he was forced to flee the city and appeal to Alfonso VI for help. The rebels invited the king of Badajoz, al-Mutawakkil, to rule Toledo. The king of Badajoz occupied Toledo in 1079, but Alfonso VI sent forces to help al-Qadir recover Toledo. Alfonso captured the fortress town of Coria, which controlled a pass from Castilian lands into the lands of the taifa of Badajoz. Since Alfonso now threatened him through Coria, al-Mutawakkil withdrew from Toledo and al-Qadir was able to return to Toledo. As the price of his help, Alfonso obtained the right to station two garrisons of his soldiers on the lands of Toledo, at al-Qadir's expense.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
        <a href="#cite_note-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A second revolt against al-Qadir took place in 1082. This time al-Qadir defeated the rebels in Toledo, chased them to Madrid, and defeated them there.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It was about this time at the latest that Alfonso VI decided to seize Toledo for himself, though some authors have argued that the plan to conquer Toledo existed by 1078.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1083, Alfonso VI campaigned against al-Mutamid, bringing his forces right up against Sevilla and reaching the city of Tarifa, with the intention of dissuading al-Mutamid from any resistance against the coming seizure of Toledo.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
        <a href="#cite_note-106">
         [106]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 1084, Alfonso set siege to Toledo, preventing the city from being supplied and also preventing agricultural work in the area. Over the winter of 1084 to 1085 the siege was maintained, while the king spent the winter north in Leon and Sahagun. In spring 1085 Alfonso personally rejoined the siege with new forces. The city soon fell and Alfonso made his triumphant entry to the city on May 24, 1085.
      </p>
      <p>
       Under Islamic Arab rule, Toledo was called
       <i>
        Ṭulayṭulah
       </i>
       . After the fall of the caliphate, Toledo was the capital city of one of the richest
       <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">
        Taifas
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">
        Al-Andalus
       </a>
       . Its population was overwhelmingly
       <a href="/wiki/Muladi" title="Muladi">
        Muladi
       </a>
       , and, because of its central location in the Iberian Peninsula, Toledo took a central position in the struggles between the Muslim and Christian rulers of northern Spain. The conquest of Toledo by
       <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_VI_of_Le%C3%B3n_and_Castile" title="Alfonso VI of León and Castile">
        Alfonso VI of Castile
       </a>
       in 1085 marked the first time a major city in Al-Andalus was captured by Christian forces; it served to sharpen the religious aspect of the Christian reconquest.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Toledo_after_the_Reconquista">
        Medieval Toledo after the Reconquista
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Toledo,_Spain&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Medieval Toledo after the Reconquista">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Braun_Toledo_UBHD.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="850" data-file-width="2086" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Braun_Toledo_UBHD.jpg/230px-Braun_Toledo_UBHD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Braun_Toledo_UBHD.jpg/345px-Braun_Toledo_UBHD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Braun_Toledo_UBHD.jpg/460px-Braun_Toledo_UBHD.jpg 2x" width="230"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Braun_Toledo_UBHD.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Toledo in the 16th century
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:232px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:El_Greco_-_View_and_Plan_of_Toledo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2959" data-file-width="5121" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/El_Greco_-_View_and_Plan_of_Toledo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/230px-El_Greco_-_View_and_Plan_of_Toledo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/El_Greco_-_View_and_Plan_of_Toledo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/345px-El_Greco_-_View_and_Plan_of_Toledo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/El_Greco_-_View_and_Plan_of_Toledo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/460px-El_Greco_-_View_and_Plan_of_Toledo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="230"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:El_Greco_-_View_and_Plan_of_Toledo_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/View_of_Toledo" title="View of Toledo">
           View of Toledo
          </a>
         </i>
         by resident
         <a href="/wiki/El_Greco" title="El Greco">
          El Greco
         </a>
         c. 1608.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       On May 25, 1085, Alfonso VI of Castile took Toledo and established direct personal control over the Moorish city from which he had been exacting tribute, ending the medieval
       <a href="/wiki/Taifa" title="Taifa">
        Taifa
       </a>
       's
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Toledo" title="Kingdom of Toledo">
        Kingdom of Toledo
       </a>
       . This was the first concrete step taken by the combined kingdom of Leon-Castile in the
       <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">
        Reconquista
       </a>
       by Christian forces. After Castilian conquest, Toledo continued to be a major cultural centre; its Arab libraries were not pillaged, and a tag-team
       <a href="/wiki/Toledo_School_of_Translators" title="Toledo School of Translators">
        translation centre
       </a>
       was established in which books in Arabic or Hebrew would be translated into Castilian by Muslim and Jewish scholars, and from Castilian into Latin by Castilian scholars, thus letting long-lost knowledge spread through Christian Europe again. Toledo served as the capital city of
       <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">
        Castile
       </a>
       intermittently (Castile did not have a permanent capital) from 1085, and the city flourished. Under the
       <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Toledo" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Toledo">
        Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Toledo
       </a>
       multiple persecutions (633, 653, 693) and stake burnings of Jews (638 CE) occurred; the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Toledo" title="Kingdom of Toledo">
        Kingdom of Toledo
       </a>
       followed up on this tradition (1368, 1391, 1449, 1486–1490 CE) including forced conversions and mass murder and the rioting and blood bath against the Jews of Toledo (1212 CE).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-STR-JewishPersecution_107-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-STR-JewishPersecution-107">
         [107]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GrosserHalperin_108-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-GrosserHalperin-108">
         [108]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       During the persecution of the Jews in the late 15th and early 16th centuries, members of the Jewish community of Toledo produced texts on their long history in Toledo. It was at this time that Don
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isaac_Abrabanel" title="Isaac Abrabanel">
        Isaac Abrabanel
       </a>
       , a prominent Jewish figure in Spain in the 15th century and one of the king's trusted courtiers who witnessed the expulsion of Jews from Spain in 1492, wrote that Toledo was named
       <i>
        Ṭulayṭulah
       </i>
       by its first Jewish inhabitants who, he stated, settled there in the 5th century BCE, and which name – by way of conjecture – may have been related to its Hebrew cognate טלטול (= wandering), on account of their wandering from Jerusalem. He says, furthermore, that the original name of the city was Pirisvalle, so-called by its early pagan inhabitants.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
        <a href="#cite_note-109">
         [109]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, there is no archaeological or historical evidence for Jewish presence in this region prior to the time of the Roman Empire; when the Romans first wrote about Toledo it was a Celtic city.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
        <a href="#cite_note-110">
         [110]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
        <a href="#cite_note-111">
         [111]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <table class="toccolours" style="width:15em;border-spacing: 0;float:right;clear:right;margin:0 0 1em 1em;">
